      <tr style="background:#f8eaba;">
       <td style="width:40%; max-height:1px; border-top:2px solid transparent; border-right:1px solid transparent;">
       </td>
       <td style="width:60%; max-height:1px; border-top:2px solid transparent; border-left:1px solid transparent;">
       </td>
      </tr>
     </table>
     <p>
      <b>
       Zinc oxide
      </b>
      is an
      <a href="/wiki/Inorganic_compound" title="Inorganic compound">
       inorganic compound
      </a>
      with the
      <a href="/wiki/Chemical_formula" title="Chemical formula">
       formula
      </a>
      <a href="/wiki/Zinc" title="Zinc">
       Zn
      </a>
      <a href="/wiki/Oxygen" title="Oxygen">
       O
      </a>
      . ZnO is a white powder that is insoluble in water, and it is widely used as an additive in numerous materials and products including rubbers, plastics, ceramics, glass, cement, lubricants,
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-wear_5-0">
       <a href="#cite_note-wear-5">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        5
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      paints, ointments, adhesives, sealants, pigments,
      <a href="/wiki/Zinc#Biological_role" title="Zinc">
       foods
      </a>
      , batteries, ferrites, fire retardants, and first-aid tapes. It occurs naturally as the mineral
      <a href="/wiki/Zincite" title="Zincite">
       zincite
      </a>
      , but most zinc oxide is produced synthetically.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Ullmann_6-0">
       <a href="#cite_note-Ullmann-6">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        6
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
     </p>
     <p>
      ZnO is a wide-bandgap semiconductor of the
      <a href="/wiki/List_of_semiconductor_materials#Group_II-VI" title="List of semiconductor materials">
       II-VI semiconductor group
      </a>
      . The native
      <a href="/wiki/Doping_(semiconductor)" title="Doping (semiconductor)">
       doping
      </a>
      of the semiconductor due to oxygen vacancies or zinc interstitials is n-type.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-ozgur_7-0">
       <a href="#cite_note-ozgur-7">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        7
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      This semiconductor has several favorable properties, including good transparency, high
      <a href="/wiki/Electron_mobility" title="Electron mobility">
       electron mobility
      </a>
      , wide
      <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Bandgap" title="Bandgap">
       bandgap
      </a>
      , and strong room-temperature
      <a href="/wiki/Luminescence" title="Luminescence">
       luminescence
      </a>
      . Those properties are used in emerging applications for transparent
      <a href="/wiki/Electrode" title="Electrode">
       electrodes
      </a>
      in
      <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Liquid_crystal_display" title="Liquid crystal display">
       liquid crystal displays
      </a>
      , in energy-saving or heat-protecting windows, and in electronics as thin-film
      <a href="/wiki/Transistor" title="Transistor">
       transistors
      </a>
      and
      <a href="/wiki/Light-emitting_diode" title="Light-emitting diode">
       light-emitting diodes
      </a>
      .
     </p>
     <p>
     </p>

         </li>

     <p>
     </p>
     <h2>
      <span class="mw-headline" id="Chemical_properties">
       Chemical properties
      </span>
      <span class="mw-editsection">
       <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
        [
       </span>
       <a href="/w/index.php?title=Zinc_oxide&amp;action=edit&amp;section=1" title="Edit section: Chemical properties">
        edit
       </a>
       <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
        ]
       </span>
      </span>
     </h2>
     <p>
      Pure ZnO presents as a white powder, but in nature it occurs as the rare mineral
      <a href="/wiki/Zincite" title="Zincite">
       zincite
      </a>
      , which usually contains manganese and other impurities that confer a yellow to red color.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-k1_8-0">
       <a href="#cite_note-k1-8">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        8
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
     </p>
     <p>
      Crystalline zinc oxide is
      <a href="/wiki/Thermochromism" title="Thermochromism">
       thermochromic
      </a>
      , changing from white to yellow when heated and in air reverting to white on cooling.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Wiberg.26Holleman_9-0">
       <a href="#cite_note-Wiberg.26Holleman-9">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        9
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      This color change is caused by a small loss of oxygen to the environment at high temperatures to form the
      <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Non-stoichiometric" title="Non-stoichiometric">
       non-stoichiometric
      </a>
      Zn
      <sub>
       1+x
      </sub>
      O, where at 800C, x = 0.00007.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Wiberg.26Holleman_9-1">
       <a href="#cite_note-Wiberg.26Holleman-9">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        9
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
     </p>
     <p>
      Zinc oxide is an
      <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Amphoteric_oxide" title="Amphoteric oxide">
       amphoteric oxide
      </a>
      . It is nearly
      <a href="/wiki/Solubility" title="Solubility">
       insoluble
      </a>
      in water, but it is soluble in (degraded by) most
      <a href="/wiki/Acid" title="Acid">
       acids
      </a>
      , such as
      <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Hydrochloric" title="Hydrochloric">
       hydrochloric
      </a>
      acid:
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-10">
       <a href="#cite_note-10">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        10
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
     </p>
     <dl>
      <dd>
       ZnO + 2 HCl  ZnCl
       <sub>
        2
       </sub>
       + H
       <sub>
        2
       </sub>
       O
      </dd>
     </dl>
     <p>
      Bases also degrade the solid to give soluble zincates:
     </p>
     <dl>
      <dd>
       ZnO + 2 NaOH + H
       <sub>
        2
       </sub>
       O  Na
       <sub>
        2
       </sub>
       [Zn(OH)
       <sub>
        4
       </sub>
       ]
      </dd>
     </dl>
     <p>
      ZnO reacts slowly with fatty acids in oils to produce the corresponding
      <a href="/wiki/Carboxylate" title="Carboxylate">
       carboxylates
      </a>
      , such as
      <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Oleate" title="Oleate">
       oleate
      </a>
      or
      <a href="/wiki/Stearate" title="Stearate">
       stearate
      </a>
      . ZnO forms cement-like products when mixed with a strong aqueous solution of
      <a href="/wiki/Zinc_chloride" title="Zinc chloride">
       zinc chloride
      </a>
      and these are best described as zinc hydroxy chlorides.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-11">
       <a href="#cite_note-11">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        11
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      This cement was used in dentistry.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Ferracane_12-0">
       <a href="#cite_note-Ferracane-12">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        12
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
     </p>
     <div class="thumb tleft">
      <div class="thumbinner" style="width:152px;">
       <a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Hopeite.jpg">
        <img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="419" data-file-width="700" height="90" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/ea/Hopeite.jpg/150px-Hopeite.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/ea/Hopeite.jpg/225px-Hopeite.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/ea/Hopeite.jpg/300px-Hopeite.jpg 2x" width="150"/>
       </a>
       <div class="thumbcaption">
        <div class="magnify">
         <a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Hopeite.jpg" title="Enlarge">
         </a>
        </div>
        Hopeite
       </div>
      </div>
     </div>
     <p>
      ZnO also forms cement-like material when treated with
      <a href="/wiki/Phosphoric_acid" title="Phosphoric acid">
       phosphoric acid
      </a>
      ; related materials are used in dentistry.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Ferracane_12-1">
       <a href="#cite_note-Ferracane-12">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        12
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      A major component of zinc phosphate cement produced by this reaction is
      <a href="/wiki/Hopeite" title="Hopeite">
       hopeite
      </a>
      , Zn
      <sub>
       3
      </sub>
      (PO
      <sub>
       4
      </sub>
      )
      <sub>
       2
      </sub>
      4H
      <sub>
       2
      </sub>
      O.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-13">
       <a href="#cite_note-13">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        13
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
     </p>
     <p>
      ZnO decomposes into zinc vapor and oxygen at around 1975C with a standard oxygen pressure. In a
      <a href="/wiki/Carbothermic_reaction" title="Carbothermic reaction">
       carbothermic reaction
      </a>
      , heating with carbon converts the oxide into zinc vapor at a much lower temperature (around 950C).
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-14">
       <a href="#cite_note-14">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        14
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
     </p>
     <dl>
      <dd>
       ZnO + C  Zn
       <sub>
        (Vapor)
       </sub>
       + CO
      </dd>
     </dl>
     <p>
      Zinc oxide can react violently with
      <a href="/wiki/Aluminium" title="Aluminium">
       aluminium
      </a>
      and
      <a href="/wiki/Magnesium" title="Magnesium">
       magnesium
      </a>
      powders, with chlorinated rubber and
      <a href="/wiki/Linseed_oil" title="Linseed oil">
       linseed oil
      </a>
      on heating causing fire and explosion hazard.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-15">
       <a href="#cite_note-15">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        15
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-16">
       <a href="#cite_note-16">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        16
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
     </p>
     <p>
      It reacts with
      <a href="/wiki/Hydrogen_sulfide" title="Hydrogen sulfide">
       hydrogen sulfide
      </a>
      to give the
      <a href="/wiki/Zinc_sulfide" title="Zinc sulfide">
       sulfide
      </a>
      . This reaction is used commercially.
     </p>
     <dl>
      <dd>
       ZnO + H
       <sub>
        2
       </sub>
       S  ZnS + H
       <sub>
        2
       </sub>
       O
      </dd>
     </dl>
     <h2>
      <span class="mw-headline" id="Physical_properties">
       Physical properties
      </span>
      <span class="mw-editsection">
       <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
        [
       </span>
       <a href="/w/index.php?title=Zinc_oxide&amp;action=edit&amp;section=2" title="Edit section: Physical properties">
        edit
       </a>
       <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
        ]
       </span>
      </span>
     </h2>
     <div class="thumb tright">
      <div class="thumbinner" style="width:152px;">
       <a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Wurtzite_polyhedra.png">
        <img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="1417" data-file-width="1417" height="150" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/8e/Wurtzite_polyhedra.png/150px-Wurtzite_polyhedra.png" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/8e/Wurtzite_polyhedra.png/225px-Wurtzite_polyhedra.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/8e/Wurtzite_polyhedra.png/300px-Wurtzite_polyhedra.png 2x" width="150"/>
       </a>
       <div class="thumbcaption">
        <div class="magnify">
         <a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Wurtzite_polyhedra.png" title="Enlarge">
         </a>
        </div>
        Wurtzite structure
       </div>
      </div>
     </div>
     <div class="thumb tright">
      <div class="thumbinner" style="width:152px;">
       <a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Sphalerite-unit-cell-depth-fade-3D-balls.png">
        <img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="1001" data-file-width="1100" height="137" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/22/Sphalerite-unit-cell-depth-fade-3D-balls.png/150px-Sphalerite-unit-cell-depth-fade-3D-balls.png" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/22/Sphalerite-unit-cell-depth-fade-3D-balls.png/225px-Sphalerite-unit-cell-depth-fade-3D-balls.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/22/Sphalerite-unit-cell-depth-fade-3D-balls.png/300px-Sphalerite-unit-cell-depth-fade-3D-balls.png 2x" width="150"/>
       </a>
       <div class="thumbcaption">
        <div class="magnify">
         <a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Sphalerite-unit-cell-depth-fade-3D-balls.png" title="Enlarge">
         </a>
        </div>
        A zincblende unit cell
       </div>
      </div>
     </div>
     <h3>
      <span class="mw-headline" id="Structure">
       Structure
      </span>
      <span class="mw-editsection">
       <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
        [
       </span>
       <a href="/w/index.php?title=Zinc_oxide&amp;action=edit&amp;section=3" title="Edit section: Structure">
        edit
       </a>
       <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
        ]
       </span>
      </span>
     </h3>
     <p>
      Zinc oxide crystallizes in two main
      <a href="/wiki/Polymorphism_(materials_science)" title="Polymorphism (materials science)">
       forms
      </a>
      , hexagonal
      <a href="/wiki/Wurtzite_crystal_structure" title="Wurtzite crystal structure">
       wurtzite
      </a>
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-fierro_17-0">
       <a href="#cite_note-fierro-17">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        17
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      and cubic
      <a href="/wiki/Cubic_crystal_system#Zincblende_structure" title="Cubic crystal system">
       zincblende
      </a>
      . The wurtzite structure is most stable at ambient conditions and thus most common. The zincblende form can be stabilized by growing ZnO on substrates with cubic lattice structure. In both cases, the zinc and oxide centers are
      <a href="/wiki/Tetrahedral_molecular_geometry" title="Tetrahedral molecular geometry">
       tetrahedral
      </a>
      , the most characteristic geometry for Zn(II). ZnO converts to the
      <a href="/wiki/Sodium_chloride" title="Sodium chloride">
       rocksalt
      </a>
      motif at relatively high pressures about 10 GPa.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-ozgur_7-1">
       <a href="#cite_note-ozgur-7">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        7
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
     </p>
     <p>
      Hexagonal and zincblende polymorphs have no
      <a href="/wiki/Reflection_symmetry" title="Reflection symmetry">
       inversion symmetry
      </a>
      (reflection of a crystal relative to any given point does not transform it into itself). This and other lattice symmetry properties result in
      <a href="/wiki/Piezoelectricity" title="Piezoelectricity">
       piezoelectricity
      </a>
      of the hexagonal and zincblende ZnO, and
      <a href="/wiki/Pyroelectricity" title="Pyroelectricity">
       pyroelectricity
      </a>
      of hexagonal ZnO.
     </p>
     <p>
      The hexagonal structure has a point group 6mm (
      <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Hermann-Mauguin_notation" title="Hermann-Mauguin notation">
       Hermann-Mauguin notation
      </a>
      ) or C
      <sub>
       6v
      </sub>
      (
      <a href="/wiki/Schoenflies_notation" title="Schoenflies notation">
       Schoenflies notation
      </a>
      ), and the
      <a href="/wiki/Space_group" title="Space group">
       space group
      </a>
      is P6
      <sub>
       3
      </sub>
      mc or C
      <sub>
       6v
      </sub>
      <sup>
       4
      </sup>
      . The lattice constants are
      <i>
       a
      </i>
      = 3.25  and
      <i>
       c
      </i>
      = 5.2 ; their ratio
      <i>
       c/a
      </i>
      ~ 1.60 is close to the ideal value for hexagonal cell
      <i>
       c/a
      </i>
      = 1.633.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-18">
       <a href="#cite_note-18">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        18
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      As in most
      <a href="/wiki/Group_(periodic_table)" title="Group (periodic table)">
       group II-VI
      </a>
      materials, the bonding in ZnO is largely
      <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Ionic_bond" title="Ionic bond">
       ionic
      </a>
      (Zn
      <sup>
       2+
      </sup>
      O
      <sup>
       2
      </sup>
      ) with the corresponding radii of 0.074nm for Zn
      <sup>
       2+
      </sup>
      and 0.140nm for O
      <sup>
       2
      </sup>
      . This property accounts for the preferential formation of wurtzite rather than zinc blende structure,
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-19">
       <a href="#cite_note-19">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        19
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      as well as the strong
      <a href="/wiki/Piezoelectricity" title="Piezoelectricity">
       piezoelectricity
      </a>
      of ZnO. Because of the polar Zn-O bonds, zinc and oxygen planes are electrically charged. To maintain electrical neutrality, those planes reconstruct at atomic level in most relative materials, but not in ZnO  its surfaces are atomically flat, stable and exhibit no reconstruction. This anomaly of ZnO is not fully explained yet.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-dutta_20-0">
       <a href="#cite_note-dutta-20">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        20
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
     </p>
     <h3>
      <span class="mw-headline" id="Mechanical_properties">
       Mechanical properties
      </span>
      <span class="mw-editsection">
       <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
        [
       </span>
       <a href="/w/index.php?title=Zinc_oxide&amp;action=edit&amp;section=4" title="Edit section: Mechanical properties">
        edit
       </a>
       <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
        ]
       </span>
      </span>
     </h3>
     <p>
      ZnO is a relatively soft material with approximate hardness of 4.5 on the
      <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Mohs_scale" title="Mohs scale">
       Mohs scale
      </a>
      .
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-wear_5-1">
       <a href="#cite_note-wear-5">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        5
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      Its elastic constants are smaller than those of relevant III-V semiconductors, such as
      <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/GaN" title="GaN">
       GaN
      </a>
      . The high heat capacity and heat conductivity, low thermal expansion and high melting temperature of ZnO are beneficial for ceramics.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-porter_21-0">
       <a href="#cite_note-porter-21">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        21
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      ZnO exhibits a very long lived optical phonon E2(low) with a lifetime as high as 133 ps at 10 K.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-22">
       <a href="#cite_note-22">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        22
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      <sup class="noprint Inline-Template" style="white-space:nowrap;">
       [
       <i>
        <a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Please_clarify" title="Wikipedia:Please clarify">
         <span title="The text near this tag may need clarification or removal of jargon. (April 2014)">
          clarification needed
         </span>
        </a>
       </i>
       ]
      </sup>
     </p>
     <p>
      Among the tetrahedrally bonded semiconductors, it has been stated that ZnO has the highest piezoelectric tensor, or at least one comparable to that of
      <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/GaN" title="GaN">
       GaN
      </a>
      and
      <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/AlN" title="AlN">
       AlN
      </a>
      .
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-23">
       <a href="#cite_note-23">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        23
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      This property makes it a technologically important material for many
      <a href="/wiki/Piezoelectricity" title="Piezoelectricity">
       piezoelectrical
      </a>
      applications, which require a large electromechanical coupling.
     </p>
     <h3>
      <span class="mw-headline" id="Electrical_properties">
       Electrical properties
      </span>
      <span class="mw-editsection">
       <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
        [
       </span>
       <a href="/w/index.php?title=Zinc_oxide&amp;action=edit&amp;section=5" title="Edit section: Electrical properties">
        edit
       </a>
       <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
        ]
       </span>
      </span>
     </h3>
     <p>
      ZnO has a relatively large
      <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Direct_bandgap" title="Direct bandgap">
       direct
      </a>
      <a href="/wiki/Band_gap" title="Band gap">
       band gap
      </a>
      of ~3.3eV at room temperature. Advantages associated with a large band gap include higher breakdown voltages, ability to sustain large electric fields, lower
      <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Electronic_noise" title="Electronic noise">
       electronic noise
      </a>
      , and high-temperature and high-power operation. The bandgap of ZnO can further be tuned to ~34eV by its alloying with
      <a href="/wiki/Magnesium_oxide" title="Magnesium oxide">
       magnesium oxide
      </a>
      or
      <a href="/wiki/Cadmium_oxide" title="Cadmium oxide">
       cadmium oxide
      </a>
      .
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-ozgur_7-2">
       <a href="#cite_note-ozgur-7">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        7
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
     </p>
     <p>
      Most ZnO has
      <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/N-type_semiconductor" title="N-type semiconductor">
       <i>
        n
       </i>
       -type
      </a>
      character, even in the absence of intentional
      <a href="/wiki/Doping_(semiconductor)" title="Doping (semiconductor)">
       doping
      </a>
      .
      <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Nonstoichiometric_compound" title="Nonstoichiometric compound">
       Nonstoichiometry
      </a>
      is typically the origin of n-type character, but the subject remains controversial.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Look1999_24-0">
       <a href="#cite_note-Look1999-24">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        24
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      An alternative explanation has been proposed, based on theoretical calculations, that unintentional substitutional hydrogen impurities are responsible.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Janotti2007_25-0">
       <a href="#cite_note-Janotti2007-25">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        25
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      Controllable n-type doping is easily achieved by substituting Zn with group-III elements such as Al, Ga, In or by substituting oxygen with group-VII elements
      <a href="/wiki/Chlorine" title="Chlorine">
       chlorine
      </a>
      or
      <a href="/wiki/Iodine" title="Iodine">
       iodine
      </a>
      .
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-26">
       <a href="#cite_note-26">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        26
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
     </p>
     <p>
      Reliable
      <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/P-type_semiconductor" title="P-type semiconductor">
       p-type
      </a>
      doping of ZnO remains difficult. This problem originates from low solubility of p-type dopants and their compensation by abundant n-type impurities. This problem is observed with
      <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/GaN" title="GaN">
       GaN
      </a>
      and
      <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/ZnSe" title="ZnSe">
       ZnSe
      </a>
      . Measurement of p-type in "intrinsically" n-type material is complicated by the inhomogeneity of samples.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-27">
       <a href="#cite_note-27">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        27
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
     </p>
     <p>
      Current limitations to p-doping does not limit electronic and optoelectronic applications of ZnO, which usually require junctions of n-type and p-type material. Known p-type dopants include group-I elements Li, Na, K; group-V elements N, P and As; as well as copper and silver. However, many of these form deep acceptors and do not produce significant p-type conduction at room temperature.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-ozgur_7-3">
       <a href="#cite_note-ozgur-7">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        7
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
     </p>
     <p>
      <a href="/wiki/Electron_mobility" title="Electron mobility">
       Electron mobility
      </a>
      of ZnO strongly varies with temperature and has a maximum of ~2000cm
      <sup>
       2
      </sup>
      /(Vs) at 80 K.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-28">
       <a href="#cite_note-28">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        28
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      Data on hole mobility are scarce with values in the range 530cm
      <sup>
       2
      </sup>
      /(Vs).
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-29">
       <a href="#cite_note-29">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        29
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
     </p>
     <h2>
      <span class="mw-headline" id="Production">
       Production
      </span>
      <span class="mw-editsection">
       <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
        [
       </span>
       <a href="/w/index.php?title=Zinc_oxide&amp;action=edit&amp;section=6" title="Edit section: Production">
        edit
       </a>
       <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
        ]
       </span>
      </span>
     </h2>
     <div class="hatnote">
      See also:
      <a href="/wiki/Zinc_smelting" title="Zinc smelting">
       Zinc smelting
      </a>
     </div>
     <p>
      For industrial use, ZnO is produced at levels of 10
      <sup>
       5
      </sup>
      tons per year
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-k1_8-1">
       <a href="#cite_note-k1-8">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        8
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      by three main processes:
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-porter_21-1">
       <a href="#cite_note-porter-21">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        21
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
     </p>
     <h3>
      <span class="mw-headline" id="Indirect_process">
       Indirect process
      </span>
      <span class="mw-editsection">
       <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
        [
       </span>
       <a href="/w/index.php?title=Zinc_oxide&amp;action=edit&amp;section=7" title="Edit section: Indirect process">
        edit
       </a>
       <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
        ]
       </span>
      </span>
     </h3>
     <p>
      In the indirect or French process, metallic zinc is melted in a graphite crucible and vaporized at temperatures above 907C (typically around 1000C). Zinc vapor reacts with the oxygen in the air to give ZnO, accompanied by a drop in its temperature and bright luminescence. Zinc oxide particles are transported into a cooling duct and collected in a bag house. This indirect method was popularized by LeClaire (France) in 1844 and therefore is commonly known as the French process. Its product normally consists of agglomerated zinc oxide particles with an average size of 0.1 to a few micrometers. By weight, most of the world's zinc oxide is manufactured via French process.
     </p>
     <h3>
      <span class="mw-headline" id="Direct_process">
       Direct process
      </span>
      <span class="mw-editsection">
       <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
        [
       </span>
       <a href="/w/index.php?title=Zinc_oxide&amp;action=edit&amp;section=8" title="Edit section: Direct process">
        edit
       </a>
       <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
        ]
       </span>
      </span>
     </h3>
     <p>
      The direct or American process starts with diverse contaminated zinc composites, such as
      <a href="/wiki/Calamine_(mineral)" title="Calamine (mineral)">
       zinc ores
      </a>
      or smelter by-products. The zinc precursors are reduced (
      <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Carbothermal_reduction" title="Carbothermal reduction">
       carbothermal reduction
      </a>
      ) by heating with a source of carbon such as
      <a href="/wiki/Anthracite" title="Anthracite">
       anthracite
      </a>
      to produce zinc vapor, which is then oxidized as in the indirect process. Because of the lower purity of the source material, the final product is also of lower quality in the direct process as compared to the indirect one.
     </p>
     <h3>
      <span class="mw-headline" id="Wet_chemical_process">
       Wet chemical process
      </span>
      <span class="mw-editsection">
       <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
        [
       </span>
       <a href="/w/index.php?title=Zinc_oxide&amp;action=edit&amp;section=9" title="Edit section: Wet chemical process">
        edit
       </a>
       <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
        ]
       </span>
      </span>
     </h3>
     <p>
      A small amount of industrial production involves wet chemical processes, which start with aqueous solutions of purified zinc salts, from which
      <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Zinc_carbonate" title="Zinc carbonate">
       zinc carbonate
      </a>
      or
      <a href="/wiki/Zinc_hydroxide" title="Zinc hydroxide">
       zinc hydroxide
      </a>
      is precipitated. The precipitate is then filtered, washed, dried and calcined at temperatures around 800C.
     </p>
     <h3>
      <span class="mw-headline" id="Laboratory_synthesis">
       Laboratory synthesis
      </span>
      <span class="mw-editsection">
       <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
        [
       </span>
       <a href="/w/index.php?title=Zinc_oxide&amp;action=edit&amp;section=10" title="Edit section: Laboratory synthesis">
        edit
       </a>
       <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
        ]
       </span>
      </span>
     </h3>
     <div class="thumb tright">
      <div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;">
       <a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Synthetic_Zincite_Crystals.jpg">
        <img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="275" data-file-width="353" height="171" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/en/thumb/1/1a/Synthetic_Zincite_Crystals.jpg/220px-Synthetic_Zincite_Crystals.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/en/thumb/1/1a/Synthetic_Zincite_Crystals.jpg/330px-Synthetic_Zincite_Crystals.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/en/1/1a/Synthetic_Zincite_Crystals.jpg 2x" width="220"/>
       </a>
       <div class="thumbcaption">
        <div class="magnify">
         <a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Synthetic_Zincite_Crystals.jpg" title="Enlarge">
         </a>
        </div>
        Synthetic ZnO crystals. Red and green color are associated with different concentrations of oxygen vacancies.
        <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-growth_30-0">
         <a href="#cite_note-growth-30">
          <span>
           [
          </span>
          30
          <span>
           ]
          </span>
         </a>
        </sup>
       </div>
      </div>
     </div>
     <p>
      A large number of specialised methods exist for producing ZnO for scientific studies and niche applications. These methods can be classified by the resulting ZnO form (bulk, thin film,
      <a href="/wiki/Nanowire" title="Nanowire">
       nanowire
      </a>
      ), temperature ("low", that is close to room temperature or "high", that is T~1000C), process type (vapor deposition or growth from solution) and other parameters.
     </p>
     <p>
      Large single crystals (many cubic centimeters) can be grown by the gas transport (vapor-phase deposition),
      <a href="/wiki/Hydrothermal_synthesis" title="Hydrothermal synthesis">
       hydrothermal synthesis
      </a>
      ,
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-dutta_20-1">
       <a href="#cite_note-dutta-20">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        20
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-growth_30-1">
       <a href="#cite_note-growth-30">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        30
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-31">
       <a href="#cite_note-31">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        31
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      or melt growth.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-melt_2-1">
       <a href="#cite_note-melt-2">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        2
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      However, because of high
      <a href="/wiki/Vapor_pressure" title="Vapor pressure">
       vapor pressure
      </a>
      of ZnO, growth from the melt is problematic. Growth by gas transport is difficult to control, leaving the hydrothermal method as a preference.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-melt_2-2">
       <a href="#cite_note-melt-2">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        2
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      Thin films can be produced by
      <a href="/wiki/Chemical_vapor_deposition" title="Chemical vapor deposition">
       chemical vapor deposition
      </a>
      ,
      <a href="/wiki/Metalorganic_vapour_phase_epitaxy" title="Metalorganic vapour phase epitaxy">
       metalorganic vapour phase epitaxy
      </a>
      ,
      <a href="/wiki/Electrophoretic_deposition" title="Electrophoretic deposition">
       electrodeposition
      </a>
      ,
      <a href="/wiki/Pulsed_laser_deposition" title="Pulsed laser deposition">
       pulsed laser deposition
      </a>
      ,
      <a href="/wiki/Sputtering" title="Sputtering">
       sputtering
      </a>
      ,
      <a href="/wiki/Sol-gel" title="Sol-gel">
       sol-gel
      </a>
      synthesis,
      <a href="/wiki/Atomic_layer_deposition" title="Atomic layer deposition">
       atomic layer deposition
      </a>
      , spray pyrolysis, etc.
     </p>
     <p>
      Ordinary white powdered zinc oxide can be produced in the laboratory by electrolyzing a solution of sodium bicarbonate with a zinc anode. Zinc hydroxide and hydrogen gas are produced. The zinc hydroxide upon heating decomposes to zinc oxide.
     </p>
     <dl>
      <dd>
       Zn + 2 H
       <sub>
        2
       </sub>
       O  Zn(OH)
       <sub>
        2
       </sub>
       + H
       <sub>
        2
       </sub>
      </dd>
     </dl>
     <dl>
      <dd>
       Zn(OH)
       <sub>
        2
       </sub>
        ZnO + H
       <sub>
        2
       </sub>
       O
      </dd>
     </dl>
     <h3>
      <span class="mw-headline" id="ZnO_nanostructures">
       ZnO nanostructures
      </span>
      <span class="mw-editsection">
       <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
        [
       </span>
       <a href="/w/index.php?title=Zinc_oxide&amp;action=edit&amp;section=11" title="Edit section: ZnO nanostructures">
        edit
       </a>
       <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
        ]
       </span>
      </span>
     </h3>
     <p>
      Nanostructures of ZnO can be synthesized into a variety of morphologies including nanowires, nanorods, tetrapods, nanobelts, nanoflowers, nanoparticles etc. Nanostructures can be obtained with most above-mentioned techniques, at certain conditions, and also with the
      <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Vapor-liquid-solid_method" title="Vapor-liquid-solid method">
       vapor-liquid-solid method
      </a>
      .
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-dutta_20-2">
       <a href="#cite_note-dutta-20">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        20
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-32">
       <a href="#cite_note-32">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        32
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
     </p>
     <p>
      <a href="/wiki/Nanorod" title="Nanorod">
       Rodlike
      </a>
      nanostructures of ZnO can be produced via aqueous methods, which are attractive for the following reasons: They are low cost, less hazardous
      <sup class="noprint Inline-Template Template-Fact" style="white-space:nowrap;">
       [
       <i>
        <a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Citation_needed" title="Wikipedia:Citation needed">
         <span title="This claim needs references to reliable sources. (March 2012)">
          citation needed
         </span>
        </a>
       </i>
       ]
      </sup>
      , and thus capable of easy scaling up; the growth occurs at a relatively low temperature, compatible with flexible organic substrates; there is no need for the use of metal catalysts, and thus it can be integrated with well-developed silicon technologies. In addition, there are a variety of parameters that can be tuned to effectively control the morphology and properties of the final product. Wet chemical methods have been demonstrated as a very powerful and versatile technique for growing one-dimensional ZnO nanostructures.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-33">
       <a href="#cite_note-33">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        33
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      The synthesis is typically carried out at temperatures of about 90C, in an equimolar aqueous solution of zinc nitrate and
      <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Hexamine" title="Hexamine">
       hexamine
      </a>
      , the latter providing the basic environment. Certain additives, such as polyethylene glycol or polyethylenimine, can improve the aspect ratio of the ZnO nanowires.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-34">
       <a href="#cite_note-34">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        34
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      Doping of the ZnO nanowires has been achieved by adding other metal nitrates to the growth solution.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-35">
       <a href="#cite_note-35">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        35
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      The morphology of the resulting nanostructures can be tuned by changing the parameters relating to the precursor composition (such as the zinc concentration and pH) or to the thermal treatment (such as the temperature and heating rate).
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-36">
       <a href="#cite_note-36">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        36
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
     </p>
     <p>
      Aligned ZnO nanowires on pre-seeded
      <a href="/wiki/Silicon" title="Silicon">
       silicon
      </a>
      ,
      <a href="/wiki/Glass" title="Glass">
       glass
      </a>
      and
      <a href="/wiki/Gallium_nitride" title="Gallium nitride">
       gallium nitride
      </a>
      substrates have been grown in aqueous solutions using aqueous zinc salts such as
      <a href="/wiki/Zinc_nitrate" title="Zinc nitrate">
       Zinc nitrate
      </a>
      and
      <a href="/wiki/Zinc_acetate" title="Zinc acetate">
       Zinc acetate
      </a>
      in basic environments.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-37">
       <a href="#cite_note-37">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        37
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      Pre-seeding substrates with ZnO creates sites for homogeneous nucleation of ZnO crystal during the synthesis. Common pre-seeding methods include in-situ thermal decomposition of
      <a href="/wiki/Zinc_acetate" title="Zinc acetate">
       zinc acetate
      </a>
      crystallites, spincoating of ZnO nanoparticles and the use of
      <a href="/wiki/Physical_vapor_deposition" title="Physical vapor deposition">
       physical vapor deposition
      </a>
      methods to deposit ZnO thin films.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-38">
       <a href="#cite_note-38">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        38
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-39">
       <a href="#cite_note-39">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        39
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      Pre-seeding can be performed in conjunction with top down patterning methods such as
      <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Electron_beam_lithography" title="Electron beam lithography">
       electron beam lithography
      </a>
      and nanosphere lithography to designate nucleation sites prior to growth. Aligned ZnO nanowires can be used in
      <a href="/wiki/Dye-sensitized_solar_cell" title="Dye-sensitized solar cell">
       dye-sensitized solar cells
      </a>
      and field emission devices.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-40">
       <a href="#cite_note-40">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        40
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-41">
       <a href="#cite_note-41">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        41
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
     </p>
     <h2>
      <span class="mw-headline" id="History">
       History
      </span>
      <span class="mw-editsection">
       <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
        [
       </span>
       <a href="/w/index.php?title=Zinc_oxide&amp;action=edit&amp;section=12" title="Edit section: History">
        edit
       </a>
       <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
        ]
       </span>
      </span>
     </h2>
     <p>
      Zinc compounds were probably used by early humans, in processed and unprocessed forms, as a paint or medicinal ointment, but their composition is uncertain. The use of
      <i>
       pushpanjan
      </i>
      , probably zinc oxide, as a salve for eyes and open wounds, is mentioned in the
      <a href="/wiki/India" title="India">
       Indian
      </a>
      medical text the
      <a href="/wiki/Charaka_Samhita" title="Charaka Samhita">
       Charaka Samhita
      </a>
      , thought to date from 500 BC or before.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-42">
       <a href="#cite_note-42">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        42
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      Zinc oxide ointment is also mentioned by the
      <a href="/wiki/Greeks" title="Greeks">
       Greek
      </a>
      physician
      <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Dioscorides" title="Dioscorides">
       Dioscorides
      </a>
      (1st century AD.)
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-c111_43-0">
       <a href="#cite_note-c111-43">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        43
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      <a href="/wiki/Avicenna" title="Avicenna">
       Avicenna
      </a>
      mentions zinc oxide in
      <i>
       <a href="/wiki/The_Canon_of_Medicine" title="The Canon of Medicine">
        The Canon of Medicine
       </a>
      </i>
      (1025 AD), which mentioned it as a preferred treatment for a variety of
      <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Skin_condition" title="Skin condition">
       skin conditions
      </a>
      , including
      <a href="/wiki/Skin_cancer" title="Skin cancer">
       skin cancer
      </a>
      . Though it is no longer used for treating skin cancer, it is still widely used to treat a variety of other skin conditions, in products such as
      <a href="/wiki/Baby_powder" title="Baby powder">
       baby powder
      </a>
      and creams against
      <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Diaper_rash" title="Diaper rash">
       diaper rashes
      </a>
      ,
      <a href="/wiki/Calamine" title="Calamine">
       calamine
      </a>
      cream, anti-
      <a href="/wiki/Dandruff" title="Dandruff">
       dandruff
      </a>
      <a href="/wiki/Shampoo" title="Shampoo">
       shampoos
      </a>
      , and
      <a href="/wiki/Antiseptic" title="Antiseptic">
       antiseptic
      </a>
      ointments.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Harding_44-0">
       <a href="#cite_note-Harding-44">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        44
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
     </p>
     <p>
      The Romans produced considerable quantities of
      <a href="/wiki/Brass" title="Brass">
       brass
      </a>
      (an alloy of
      <a href="/wiki/Zinc" title="Zinc">
       zinc
      </a>
      and
      <a href="/wiki/Copper" title="Copper">
       copper
      </a>
      ) as early as 200 BC by a cementation process where copper was reacted with zinc oxide.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-45">
       <a href="#cite_note-45">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        45
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      The zinc oxide is thought to have been produced by heating zinc ore in a shaft furnace. This liberated metallic zinc as a vapor, which then ascended the flue and condensed as the oxide. This process was described by
      <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Dioscorides" title="Dioscorides">
       Dioscorides
      </a>
      in the 1st century AD.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-46">
       <a href="#cite_note-46">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        46
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      Zinc oxide has also been recovered from zinc mines at Zawar in
      <a href="/wiki/India" title="India">
       India
      </a>
      , dating from the second half of the first millennium BC. This was presumably also made in the same way and used to produce brass.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-c111_43-1">
       <a href="#cite_note-c111-43">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        43
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
     </p>
     <p>
      From the 12th to the 16th century zinc and zinc oxide were recognized and produced in
      <a href="/wiki/India" title="India">
       India
      </a>
      using a primitive form of the direct synthesis process. From India, zinc manufacture moved to
      <a href="/wiki/China" title="China">
       China
      </a>
      in the 17th century. In 1743, the first European zinc smelter was established in
      <a href="/wiki/Bristol" title="Bristol">
       Bristol
      </a>
      ,
      <a href="/wiki/United_Kingdom" title="United Kingdom">
       United Kingdom
      </a>
      .
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-47">
       <a href="#cite_note-47">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        47
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
     </p>
     <p>
      The main usage of zinc oxide (zinc white) was in paints and as an additive to ointments. Zinc white was accepted as a pigment in oil paintings by 1834 but it did not mix well with oil. This problem was solved by optimizing the synthesis of ZnO. In 1845, LeClaire in Paris was producing the oil paint on a large scale, and by 1850, zinc white was being manufactured throughout Europe. The success of zinc white paint was due to its advantages over the traditional white lead: zinc white is essentially permanent in sunlight, it is not blackened by sulfur-bearing air, it is non-toxic and more economical. Because zinc white is so "clean" it is valuable for making tints with other colors; however, it makes a rather brittle dry film when unmixed with other colors. For example, during the late 1890s and early 1900s, some artists used zinc white as a ground for their oil paintings. All those paintings developed cracks over the years.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-48">
       <a href="#cite_note-48">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        48
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
     </p>
     <p>
      In recent times, most zinc oxide was used in the rubber industry (see applications above). In the 1970s, the second largest application of ZnO was photocopying. High-quality ZnO produced by the "French process" was added to photocopying paper as a filler. This application was soon displaced.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-porter_21-2">
       <a href="#cite_note-porter-21">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        21
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
     </p>
     <h2>
      <span class="mw-headline" id="Applications">
       Applications
      </span>
      <span class="mw-editsection">
       <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
        [
       </span>
       <a href="/w/index.php?title=Zinc_oxide&amp;action=edit&amp;section=13" title="Edit section: Applications">
        edit
       </a>
       <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
        ]
       </span>
      </span>
     </h2>
     <p>
      The applications of zinc oxide powder are numerous, and the principal ones are summarized below. Most applications exploit the reactivity of the oxide as a precursor to other zinc compounds. For material science applications, zinc oxide has high
      <a href="/wiki/Refractive_index" title="Refractive index">
       refractive index
      </a>
      , high thermal conductivity, binding, antibacterial and UV-protection properties. Consequently, it is added into materials and products including plastics, ceramics, glass, cement,
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-49">
       <a href="#cite_note-49">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        49
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      rubber, lubricants,
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-wear_5-2">
       <a href="#cite_note-wear-5">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        5
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      paints, ointments, adhesive, sealants,
      <a href="/wiki/Concrete" title="Concrete">
       concrete
      </a>
      manufacturing, pigments, foods, batteries, ferrites, fire retardants, etc.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-ap2_50-0">
       <a href="#cite_note-ap2-50">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        50
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
     </p>
     <h3>
      <span class="mw-headline" id="Rubber_manufacture">
       Rubber manufacture
      </span>
      <span class="mw-editsection">
       <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
        [
       </span>
       <a href="/w/index.php?title=Zinc_oxide&amp;action=edit&amp;section=14" title="Edit section: Rubber manufacture">
        edit
       </a>
       <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
        ]
       </span>
      </span>
     </h3>
     <p>
      Between 50% and 60% of ZnO use is in the rubber industry.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Moezzi_2012_1.E2.80.9322_51-0">
       <a href="#cite_note-Moezzi_2012_1.E2.80.9322-51">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        51
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      Zinc oxide along with
      <a href="/wiki/Stearic_acid" title="Stearic acid">
       stearic acid
      </a>
      is used in the
      <a href="/wiki/Vulcanization" title="Vulcanization">
       vulcanization
      </a>
      of rubber
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-porter_21-3">
       <a href="#cite_note-porter-21">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        21
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-ap1_52-0">
       <a href="#cite_note-ap1-52">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        52
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-53">
       <a href="#cite_note-53">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        53
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      ZnO additive also protect rubber from fungi (see medical applications) and UV light.
     </p>
     <h3>
      <span class="mw-headline" id="Ceramic_industry">
       Ceramic industry
      </span>
      <span class="mw-editsection">
       <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
        [
       </span>
       <a href="/w/index.php?title=Zinc_oxide&amp;action=edit&amp;section=15" title="Edit section: Ceramic industry">
        edit
       </a>
       <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
        ]
       </span>
      </span>
     </h3>
     <p>
      Ceramic industry consumes a significant amount of zinc oxide, in particular in ceramic glaze and frit compositions. The relatively high heat capacity, thermal conductivity and high temperature stability of ZnO coupled with a comparatively low coefcient of expansion are desirable properties in the production of ceramics. ZnO affects the melting point and optical properties of the glazes, enamels, and ceramic formulations. Zinc oxide as a low expansion, secondary ux improves the elasticity of glazes by reducing the change in viscosity as a function of temperature and helps prevent crazing and shivering. By substituting ZnO for BaO and PbO, the heat capacity is decreased and the thermal conductivity is increased. Zinc in small amounts improves the development of glossy and brilliant surfaces. However in moderate to high amounts, it produces matte and crystalline surfaces. With regard to color, zinc has a complicated inuence.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Moezzi_2012_1.E2.80.9322_51-1">
       <a href="#cite_note-Moezzi_2012_1.E2.80.9322-51">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        51
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
     </p>
     <h3>
      <span class="mw-headline" id="Medicine">
       Medicine
      </span>
      <span class="mw-editsection">
       <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
        [
       </span>
       <a href="/w/index.php?title=Zinc_oxide&amp;action=edit&amp;section=16" title="Edit section: Medicine">
        edit
       </a>
       <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
        ]
       </span>
      </span>
     </h3>
     <p>
      Zinc oxide as a mixture with about 0.5%
      <a href="/wiki/Iron(III)_oxide" title="Iron(III) oxide">
       iron(III) oxide
      </a>
      (Fe
      <sub>
       2
      </sub>
      O
      <sub>
       3
      </sub>
      ) is called
      <a href="/wiki/Calamine" title="Calamine">
       calamine
      </a>
      and is used in calamine lotion. Two minerals,
      <a href="/wiki/Zincite" title="Zincite">
       zincite
      </a>
      and
      <a href="/wiki/Hemimorphite" title="Hemimorphite">
       hemimorphite
      </a>
      , have been historically called
      <a href="/wiki/Calamine_(mineral)" title="Calamine (mineral)">
       calamine
      </a>
      . When mixed with
      <a href="/wiki/Eugenol" title="Eugenol">
       eugenol
      </a>
      , a
      <a href="/wiki/Ligand" title="Ligand">
       ligand
      </a>
      ,
      <a href="/wiki/Zinc_oxide_eugenol" title="Zinc oxide eugenol">
       zinc oxide eugenol
      </a>
      is formed, which has applications as a
      <a href="/wiki/Dental_restoration" title="Dental restoration">
       restorative
      </a>
      and
      <a href="/wiki/Prosthodontics" title="Prosthodontics">
       prosthodontic
      </a>
      in
      <a href="/wiki/Dentistry" title="Dentistry">
       dentistry
      </a>
      .
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Ferracane_12-2">
       <a href="#cite_note-Ferracane-12">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        12
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-54">
       <a href="#cite_note-54">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        54
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
     </p>
     <p>
      Reflecting the basic properties of ZnO, fine particles of the oxide have deodorizing and antibacterial
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-55">
       <a href="#cite_note-55">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        55
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      properties and for that reason are added into materials including cotton fabric, rubber, oral care products,
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-56">
       <a href="#cite_note-56">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        56
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-57">
       <a href="#cite_note-57">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        57
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      and food packaging.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-58">
       <a href="#cite_note-58">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        58
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-59">
       <a href="#cite_note-59">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        59
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      Enhanced antibacterial action of fine particles compared to bulk material is not exclusive to ZnO and is observed for other materials, such as
      <a href="/wiki/Silver" title="Silver">
       silver
      </a>
      .
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-60">
       <a href="#cite_note-60">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        60
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      This property is due to the increased surface area of the fine particles.
     </p>
     <p>
      Zinc oxide is widely used to treat a variety of other skin conditions, in products such as
      <a href="/wiki/Baby_powder" title="Baby powder">
       baby powder
      </a>
      and
      <a href="/wiki/Barrier_cream" title="Barrier cream">
       barrier creams
      </a>
      to treat
      <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Diaper_rash" title="Diaper rash">
       diaper rashes
      </a>
      ,
      <a href="/wiki/Calamine" title="Calamine">
       calamine
      </a>
      cream, anti-
      <a href="/wiki/Dandruff" title="Dandruff">
       dandruff
      </a>
      <a href="/wiki/Shampoo" title="Shampoo">
       shampoos
      </a>
      , and
      <a href="/wiki/Antiseptic" title="Antiseptic">
       antiseptic
      </a>
      ointments.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Harding_44-1">
       <a href="#cite_note-Harding-44">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        44
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-61">
       <a href="#cite_note-61">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        61
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      It is also a component in tape (called "zinc oxide tape") used by athletes as a bandage to prevent soft tissue damage during workouts.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-62">
       <a href="#cite_note-62">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        62
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
     </p>
     <p>
      Zinc oxide can be used in ointments, creams, and
      <a href="/wiki/Lotion" title="Lotion">
       lotions
      </a>
      to protect against
      <a href="/wiki/Sunburn" title="Sunburn">
       sunburn
      </a>
      and other damage to the skin caused by
      <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Ultraviolet_light" title="Ultraviolet light">
       ultraviolet light
      </a>
      (see
      <a href="/wiki/Sunscreen" title="Sunscreen">
       sunscreen
      </a>
      ). It is the broadest spectrum UVA and UVB reflector that is approved for use as a sunscreen by the FDA,
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-63">
       <a href="#cite_note-63">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        63
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      and is completely photostable.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-64">
       <a href="#cite_note-64">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        64
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      When used as an ingredient in
      <a href="/wiki/Sunscreen" title="Sunscreen">
       sunscreen
      </a>
      , zinc oxide blocks both
      <a href="/wiki/Ultraviolet#Subtypes" title="Ultraviolet">
       UVA
      </a>
      (320400nm) and
      <a href="/wiki/Ultraviolet#Subtypes" title="Ultraviolet">
       UVB
      </a>
      (280320nm) rays of
      <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Ultraviolet_light" title="Ultraviolet light">
       ultraviolet light
      </a>
      . Zinc oxide and the other most common physical
      <a href="/wiki/Sunscreen" title="Sunscreen">
       sunscreen
      </a>
      ,
      <a href="/wiki/Titanium_dioxide" title="Titanium dioxide">
       titanium dioxide
      </a>
      , are considered to be nonirritating, nonallergenic, and non-
      <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Comedogenic" title="Comedogenic">
       comedogenic
      </a>
      .
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-65">
       <a href="#cite_note-65">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        65
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      Zinc from zinc oxide is, however, slightly absorbed into the skin
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-66">
       <a href="#cite_note-66">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        66
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
     </p>
     <p>
      Many sunscreens use nanoparticles of zinc oxide (along with nanoparticles of titanium dioxide) because such small particles do not scatter light and therefore do not appear white. There has been concern that they might be absorbed into the skin,
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-67">
       <a href="#cite_note-67">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        67
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-68">
       <a href="#cite_note-68">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        68
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      a study published in 2010 found a 0.23% to 1.31% (mean 0.42%) of blood zinc levels in venous blood samples could be traced to zinc from ZnO nanoparticles applied to human skin for 5 days, and traces were also found in urine samples.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-69">
       <a href="#cite_note-69">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        69
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      In contrast, a comprehensive review of the medical literature from 2011 says that no evidence of systemic absorption can be found in the literature.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-70">
       <a href="#cite_note-70">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        70
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
     </p>
     <p>
      Zinc oxide
      <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Nanoparticles" title="Nanoparticles">
       nanoparticles
      </a>
      can enhance the antibacterial activity of
      <a href="/wiki/Ciprofloxacin" title="Ciprofloxacin">
       Ciprofloxacin
      </a>
      . It has been shown that nano ZnO which has the average size between 20nm and 45nm can enhance the antibacterial activity of
      <a href="/wiki/Ciprofloxacin" title="Ciprofloxacin">
       Ciprofloxacin
      </a>
      against
      <i>
       <a href="/wiki/Staphylococcus_aureus" title="Staphylococcus aureus">
        Staphylococcus aureus
       </a>
      </i>
      and
      <i>
       <a href="/wiki/Escherichia_coli" title="Escherichia coli">
        Escherichia coli
       </a>
      </i>
      <a href="/wiki/In_Vitro" title="In Vitro">
       in Vitro
      </a>
      . The enhancing effect of this nanomaterial is concentration-dependent against all test strains. This effect may be due to two reasons. First, Zinc Oxide nanoparticles can interfere with NorA protein. NorA is a protein which is developed for conferring
      <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Antibiotic_resistance" title="Antibiotic resistance">
       resistance
      </a>
      in bacteria and has pumping activity that mediate the
      <a href="/wiki/Efflux_(microbiology)" title="Efflux (microbiology)">
       effluxing
      </a>
      of hydrophilic fluroquinolones from a cell. Second, Zinc Oxide nanoparticles can interfere with Omf protein. Omf is a
      <a href="/wiki/Membrane_protein" title="Membrane protein">
       membrane protein
      </a>
      that is responsible for the permeation of
      <a href="/wiki/Quinolone" title="Quinolone">
       quinolones
      </a>
      into the cell.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-71">
       <a href="#cite_note-71">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        71
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
     </p>
     <h3>
      <span class="mw-headline" id="Cigarette_filters">
       Cigarette filters
      </span>
      <span class="mw-editsection">
       <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
        [
       </span>
       <a href="/w/index.php?title=Zinc_oxide&amp;action=edit&amp;section=17" title="Edit section: Cigarette filters">
        edit
       </a>
       <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
        ]
       </span>
      </span>
     </h3>
     <p>
      Zinc oxide is a constituent of
      <a href="/wiki/Cigarette_filter" title="Cigarette filter">
       cigarette filters
      </a>
      . A filter consisting of charcoal impregnated with zinc oxide and iron oxide removes significant amounts of
      <a href="/wiki/Hydrogen_cyanide" title="Hydrogen cyanide">
       HCN
      </a>
      and
      <a href="/wiki/Hydrogen_sulfide" title="Hydrogen sulfide">
       H
       <sub>
        2
       </sub>
       S
      </a>
      from tobacco smoke without affecting its flavor.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-ap2_50-1">
       <a href="#cite_note-ap2-50">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        50
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
     </p>
     <h3>
      <span class="mw-headline" id="Food_additive">
       Food additive
      </span>
      <span class="mw-editsection">
       <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
        [
       </span>
       <a href="/w/index.php?title=Zinc_oxide&amp;action=edit&amp;section=18" title="Edit section: Food additive">
        edit
       </a>
       <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
        ]
       </span>
      </span>
     </h3>
     <p>
      Zinc oxide is added to many food products, including
      <a href="/wiki/Breakfast_cereal" title="Breakfast cereal">
       breakfast cereals
      </a>
      , as a source of zinc,
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-72">
       <a href="#cite_note-72">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        72
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      a necessary
      <a href="/wiki/Nutrient" title="Nutrient">
       nutrient
      </a>
      . (
      <a href="/wiki/Zinc_sulfate" title="Zinc sulfate">
       Zinc sulfate
      </a>
      is also used for the same purpose.) Some prepackaged foods also include trace amounts of ZnO even if it is not intended as a nutrient.
     </p>
     <p>
      Zinc oxide was linked to dioxin contamination in pork exports in the
      <a href="/wiki/2008_Chilean_pork_crisis" title="2008 Chilean pork crisis">
       2008 Chilean pork crisis
      </a>
      . The contamination was found to be due to dioxin contaminated zinc oxide used in pig feed.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-73">
       <a href="#cite_note-73">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        73
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
     </p>
     <h3>
      <span class="mw-headline" id="Pigment">
       Pigment
      </span>
      <span class="mw-editsection">
       <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
        [
       </span>
       <a href="/w/index.php?title=Zinc_oxide&amp;action=edit&amp;section=19" title="Edit section: Pigment">
        edit
       </a>
       <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
        ]
       </span>
      </span>
     </h3>
     <p>
      Zinc white is used as a pigment in
      <a href="/wiki/Paint" title="Paint">
       paints
      </a>
      and is more opaque than
      <a href="/wiki/Lithopone" title="Lithopone">
       lithopone
      </a>
      , but less opaque than
      <a href="/wiki/Titanium_dioxide" title="Titanium dioxide">
       titanium dioxide
      </a>
      .
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Ullmann_6-1">
       <a href="#cite_note-Ullmann-6">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        6
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      It is also used in coatings for paper. Chinese white is a special grade of zinc white used in artists'
      <a href="/wiki/Pigment" title="Pigment">
       pigments
      </a>
      . It is also a main ingredient of mineral makeup (CI 77947).
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-74">
       <a href="#cite_note-74">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        74
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
     </p>
     <h3>
      <span class="mw-headline" id="UV_absorber">
       UV absorber
      </span>
      <span class="mw-editsection">
       <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
        [
       </span>
       <a href="/w/index.php?title=Zinc_oxide&amp;action=edit&amp;section=20" title="Edit section: UV absorber">
        edit
       </a>
       <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
        ]
       </span>
      </span>
     </h3>
     <p>
      Micronized and nano-scale zinc oxide and titanium dioxide provide strong protection against
      <a href="/wiki/Ultraviolet#Subtypes" title="Ultraviolet">
       UVA
      </a>
      <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Ultraviolet_radiation" title="Ultraviolet radiation">
       ultraviolet radiation
      </a>
      , and are used in
      <a href="/wiki/Sunscreen" title="Sunscreen">
       suntan lotion
      </a>
      ,
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-75">
       <a href="#cite_note-75">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        75
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      and also in UV-blocking
      <a href="/wiki/Sunglasses#Space" title="Sunglasses">
       sunglasses
      </a>
      for use in space and for protection when
      <a href="/wiki/Welding" title="Welding">
       welding
      </a>
      , following research by scientists at
      <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/JPL" title="JPL">
       JPL
      </a>
      .
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-76">
       <a href="#cite_note-76">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        76
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
     </p>
     <h3>
      <span class="mw-headline" id="Coatings">
       Coatings
      </span>
      <span class="mw-editsection">
       <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
        [
       </span>
       <a href="/w/index.php?title=Zinc_oxide&amp;action=edit&amp;section=21" title="Edit section: Coatings">
        edit
       </a>
       <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
        ]
       </span>
      </span>
     </h3>
     <p>
      Paints containing zinc oxide powder have long been utilized as anticorrosive coatings for metals. They are especially effective for galvanized iron. Iron is difficult to protect because its reactivity with organic coatings leads to brittleness and lack of adhesion. Zinc oxide paints retain their flexibility and adherence on such surfaces for many years.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-ap2_50-2">
       <a href="#cite_note-ap2-50">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        50
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
     </p>
     <p>
      ZnO highly n-type doped with Al, Ga, or In is transparent and conductive (
      <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Transparency_(optics)" title="Transparency (optics)">
       transparency
      </a>
      ~90%, lowest
      <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Resistivity" title="Resistivity">
       resistivity
      </a>
      ~10
      <sup>
       4
      </sup>
      cm
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-mende_77-0">
       <a href="#cite_note-mende-77">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        77
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      ). ZnO:Al coatings are used for energy-saving or heat-protecting windows. The coating lets the visible part of the spectrum in but either reflects the infrared (IR) radiation back into the room (energy saving) or does not let the IR radiation into the room (heat protection), depending on which side of the window has the coating.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-k1_8-2">
       <a href="#cite_note-k1-8">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        8
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
     </p>
     <p>
      Plastics, such as
      <a href="/wiki/Polyethylene_naphthalate" title="Polyethylene naphthalate">
       polyethylene naphthalate
      </a>
      (PEN), can be protected by applying zinc oxide coating. The coating reduces the diffusion of oxygen with PEN.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-78">
       <a href="#cite_note-78">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        78
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      Zinc oxide layers can also be used on
      <a href="/wiki/Polycarbonate" title="Polycarbonate">
       polycarbonate
      </a>
      (PC) in outdoor applications. The coating protects PC from solar radiation and decreases the oxidation rate and photo-yellowing of PC.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-79">
       <a href="#cite_note-79">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        79
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
     </p>
     <h3>
      <span class="mw-headline" id="Corrosion_prevention_in_nuclear_reactors">
       Corrosion prevention in nuclear reactors
      </span>
      <span class="mw-editsection">
       <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
        [
       </span>
       <a href="/w/index.php?title=Zinc_oxide&amp;action=edit&amp;section=22" title="Edit section: Corrosion prevention in nuclear reactors">
        edit
       </a>
       <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
        ]
       </span>
      </span>
     </h3>
     <div class="hatnote relarticle mainarticle">
      Main article:
      <a href="/wiki/Depleted_zinc_oxide" title="Depleted zinc oxide">
       Depleted zinc oxide
      </a>
     </div>
     <p>
      Zinc oxide depleted in the
      <a href="/wiki/Isotopes_of_zinc" title="Isotopes of zinc">
       zinc isotope
      </a>
      with the
      <a href="/wiki/Atomic_mass" title="Atomic mass">
       atomic mass
      </a>
      64 is used in corrosion prevention in nuclear
      <a href="/wiki/Pressurized_water_reactor" title="Pressurized water reactor">
       pressurized water reactors
      </a>
      . The depletion is necessary, because
      <sup>
       64
      </sup>
      Zn is
      <a href="/wiki/Neutron_activation" title="Neutron activation">
       transformed
      </a>
      into radioactive
      <sup>
       65
      </sup>
      Zn under irradiation by the reactor neutrons.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-80">
       <a href="#cite_note-80">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        80
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
     </p>
     <h3>
      <span class="mw-headline" id="Methane_reforming">
       Methane reforming
      </span>
      <span class="mw-editsection">
       <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
        [
       </span>
       <a href="/w/index.php?title=Zinc_oxide&amp;action=edit&amp;section=23" title="Edit section: Methane reforming">
        edit
       </a>
       <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
        ]
       </span>
      </span>
     </h3>
     <p>
      Zinc oxide (ZnO) is used as a pretreatment step to remove
      <a href="/wiki/Hydrogen_sulfide" title="Hydrogen sulfide">
       hydrogen sulfide
      </a>
      (H
      <sub>
       2
      </sub>
      S) from
      <a href="/wiki/Natural_gas" title="Natural gas">
       natural gas
      </a>
      following
      <a href="/wiki/Hydrogenation" title="Hydrogenation">
       hydrogenation
      </a>
      of any
      <a href="/wiki/Sulfur" title="Sulfur">
       sulfur
      </a>
      compounds prior to a
      <a href="/wiki/Methane_reformer" title="Methane reformer">
       methane reformer
      </a>
      , which can poison the catalyst. At temperatures between about 230430C (446806F), H
      <sub>
       2
      </sub>
      S is converted to
      <a href="/wiki/Water" title="Water">
       water
      </a>
      by the following reaction:
     </p>
     <dl>
      <dd>
       H
       <sub>
        2
       </sub>
       S + ZnO  H
       <sub>
        2
       </sub>
       O + ZnS
      </dd>
     </dl>
     <p>
      The
      <a href="/wiki/Zinc_sulfide" title="Zinc sulfide">
       zinc sulfide
      </a>
      (ZnS) is replaced with fresh zinc oxide when the zinc oxide has been consumed.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-81">
       <a href="#cite_note-81">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        81
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
     </p>
     <h2>
