      <span class="mw-headline" id="Potential_applications">
       Potential applications
      </span>
      <span class="mw-editsection">
       <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
        [
       </span>
       <a href="/w/index.php?title=Zinc_oxide&amp;action=edit&amp;section=24" title="Edit section: Potential applications">
        edit
       </a>
       <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
        ]
       </span>
      </span>
     </h2>
     <h3>
      <span class="mw-headline" id="Electronics">
       Electronics
      </span>
      <span class="mw-editsection">
       <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
        [
       </span>
       <a href="/w/index.php?title=Zinc_oxide&amp;action=edit&amp;section=25" title="Edit section: Electronics">
        edit
       </a>
       <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
        ]
       </span>
      </span>
     </h3>
     <p>
      ZnO has wide
      <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Direct_band_gap" title="Direct band gap">
       direct band gap
      </a>
      (3.37 eV or 375nm at room temperature). Therefore, its most common potential applications are in laser diodes and
      <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/LED" title="LED">
       light emitting diodes
      </a>
      (LEDs).
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-82">
       <a href="#cite_note-82">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        82
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      Some optoelectronic applications of ZnO overlap with that of
      <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/GaN" title="GaN">
       GaN
      </a>
      , which has a similar bandgap (~3.4 eV at room temperature). Compared to GaN, ZnO has a larger exciton binding energy (~60 meV, 2.4 times of the room-temperature thermal energy), which results in bright room-temperature emission from ZnO. ZnO can be combined with GaN for LED-applications. For instance as TCO layer and ZnO nanostructures provide better light outcoupling.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-83">
       <a href="#cite_note-83">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        83
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      Other properties of ZnO favorable for electronic applications include its stability to high-energy radiation and to wet chemical etching.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-84">
       <a href="#cite_note-84">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        84
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      Radiation resistance
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-85">
       <a href="#cite_note-85">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        85
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      makes ZnO a suitable candidate for space applications. ZnO is the most promising candidate in the field of
      <a href="/wiki/Random_laser" title="Random laser">
       random lasers
      </a>
      to produce an electronically pumped UV laser source.
     </p>
     <p>
      The pointed tips of ZnO nanorods result in a strong enhancement of an electric field. Therefore, they can be used as
      <a href="/wiki/Field_electron_emission" title="Field electron emission">
       field emitters
      </a>
      .
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-86">
       <a href="#cite_note-86">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        86
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
     </p>
     <p>
      Aluminium-doped ZnO layers are used as a transparent
      <a href="/wiki/Electrode" title="Electrode">
       electrodes
      </a>
      . The constituents Zn and Al are much cheaper and less toxic compared to the generally used
      <a href="/wiki/Indium_tin_oxide" title="Indium tin oxide">
       indium tin oxide
      </a>
      (ITO). One application which has begun to be commercially available is the use of ZnO as the front contact for solar cells or of
      <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Liquid_crystal_display" title="Liquid crystal display">
       liquid crystal displays
      </a>
      .
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-87">
       <a href="#cite_note-87">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        87
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
     </p>
     <p>
      Transparent thin-film
      <a href="/wiki/Transistor" title="Transistor">
       transistors
      </a>
      (TTFT) can be produced with ZnO. As field-effect transistors, they even may not need a pn junction,
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-88">
       <a href="#cite_note-88">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        88
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      thus avoiding the p-type doping problem of ZnO. Some of the field-effect transistors even use ZnO nanorods as conducting channels.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-89">
       <a href="#cite_note-89">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        89
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
     </p>
     <h3>
      <span class="mw-headline" id="Zinc_oxide_nanorod_sensor">
       Zinc oxide nanorod sensor
      </span>
      <span class="mw-editsection">
       <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
        [
       </span>
       <a href="/w/index.php?title=Zinc_oxide&amp;action=edit&amp;section=26" title="Edit section: Zinc oxide nanorod sensor">
        edit
       </a>
       <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
        ]
       </span>
      </span>
     </h3>
     <p>
      <a href="/wiki/Zinc_oxide_nanorod_sensor" title="Zinc oxide nanorod sensor">
       Zinc oxide nanorod sensors
      </a>
      are devices detecting changes in
      <a href="/wiki/Electric_current" title="Electric current">
       electric current
      </a>
      passing through zinc oxide
      <a href="/wiki/Nanowire" title="Nanowire">
       nanowires
      </a>
      due to
      <a href="/wiki/Adsorption" title="Adsorption">
       adsorption
      </a>
      of gas molecules. Selectivity to hydrogen gas was achieved by sputtering Pd clusters on the nanorod surface. The addition of Pd appears to be effective in the catalytic dissociation of hydrogen molecules into atomic hydrogen, increasing the sensitivity of the sensor device. The sensor detects hydrogen concentrations down to 10 parts per million at room temperature, whereas there is no response to oxygen.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-90">
       <a href="#cite_note-90">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        90
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-91">
       <a href="#cite_note-91">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        91
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
     </p>
     <h3>
      <span class="mw-headline" id="Spintronics">
       Spintronics
      </span>
      <span class="mw-editsection">
       <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
        [
       </span>
       <a href="/w/index.php?title=Zinc_oxide&amp;action=edit&amp;section=27" title="Edit section: Spintronics">
        edit
       </a>
       <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
        ]
       </span>
      </span>
     </h3>
     <p>
      ZnO has also been considered for
      <a href="/wiki/Spintronics" title="Spintronics">
       spintronics
      </a>
      applications: if doped with 110% of magnetic ions (Mn, Fe, Co, V, etc.), ZnO could become
      <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Ferromagnetic" title="Ferromagnetic">
       ferromagnetic
      </a>
      , even at room temperature. Such room temperature
      <a href="/wiki/Ferromagnetism" title="Ferromagnetism">
       ferromagnetism
      </a>
      in ZnO:Mn has been observed,
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-92">
       <a href="#cite_note-92">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        92
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      but it is not clear yet whether it originates from the matrix itself or from secondary oxide phases.
     </p>
     <h3>
      <span class="mw-headline" id="Piezoelectricity">
       Piezoelectricity
      </span>
      <span class="mw-editsection">
       <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
        [
       </span>
       <a href="/w/index.php?title=Zinc_oxide&amp;action=edit&amp;section=28" title="Edit section: Piezoelectricity">
        edit
       </a>
       <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
        ]
       </span>
      </span>
     </h3>
     <p>
      The
      <a href="/wiki/Piezoelectricity" title="Piezoelectricity">
       piezoelectricity
      </a>
      in
      <a href="/wiki/Textile" title="Textile">
       textile
      </a>
      fibers
      <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Coated" title="Coated">
       coated
      </a>
      in ZnO have been shown capable of fabricating "self-powered nanosystems" with everyday mechanical stress from wind or body movements.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-93">
       <a href="#cite_note-93">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        93
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-94">
       <a href="#cite_note-94">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        94
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
     </p>
     <p>
      In 2008 the
      <i>
       Center for Nanostructure Characterization
      </i>
      at the
      <a href="/wiki/Georgia_Institute_of_Technology" title="Georgia Institute of Technology">
       Georgia Institute of Technology
      </a>
      reported producing an electricity generating device (called flexible charge pump generator) delivering alternating current by stretching and releasing zinc oxide nanowires. This mini-generator creates an oscillating voltage up to 45 millivolts, converting close to seven percent of the applied mechanical energy into electricity. Researchers used wires with lengths of 0.20.3mm and diameters of three to five micrometers, but the device could be scaled down to smaller size.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-95">
       <a href="#cite_note-95">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        95
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
     </p>
     <h2>
      <span class="mw-headline" id="Safety">
       Safety
      </span>
      <span class="mw-editsection">
       <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
        [
       </span>
       <a href="/w/index.php?title=Zinc_oxide&amp;action=edit&amp;section=29" title="Edit section: Safety">
        edit
       </a>
       <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
        ]
       </span>
      </span>
     </h2>
     <p>
      As a food additive, zinc oxide is on the U.S. FDA's list of
      <a href="/wiki/Generally_recognized_as_safe" title="Generally recognized as safe">
       generally recognized as safe
      </a>
      , or GRAS, substances.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-96">
       <a href="#cite_note-96">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        96
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
     </p>
     <p>
      Zinc oxide itself is non-toxic; however it is hazardous to inhale zinc oxide fumes, as generated when zinc or zinc alloys are melted and oxidized at high temperature. This problem occurs while melting
      <a href="/wiki/Brass" title="Brass">
       brass
      </a>
      because the melting point of brass is close to the boiling point of zinc.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-97">
       <a href="#cite_note-97">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        97
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      Exposure to zinc oxide in the air, which also occurs while welding galvanized (zinc plated)
      <a href="/wiki/Steel" title="Steel">
       steel
      </a>
      , can result in a nervous malady called
      <a href="/wiki/Metal_fume_fever" title="Metal fume fever">
       metal fume fever
      </a>
      . For this reason, typically galvanized steel is not welded, or the zinc is removed first.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-98">
       <a href="#cite_note-98">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        98
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
     </p>

          </th>
